Master Thesis
Transfer functions of a large containership
Kasper Fønss Bach
March 2015

==PAGE:3==
Technical University of Denmark

==PAGE:4==
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The focus on application of onboard, real time operator guidance for ships in terms of decision support
systems has been increasing in recent years. Such systems can provide the master of a ship with advices
to course and/or speed changes relative to waves, to minimize the change for non-desired eﬀects such as
shift or loss of cargo. Such systems rely heavily on accuracy of theoretical calculated transfer functions to
predict the responses that cause these non-desired eﬀects.
A method for improving these theoretical calculated transfer functions by use of full-scale measurements
are developed. The method utilizes measured full-scale responses and wave radar images to derive full-scale
transfer functions. These transfer functions are used to determine the uncertainty of the theoretical
transfer functions. This uncertainty is quantiﬁed by a so-called model uncertainty factor.
A quadratic regression analysis is performed to quantify the eﬀect of draft, speed and relative wave heading
on model uncertainty factor. This leads to a mathematical model of the uncertainty factor, which can be
used to improve theoretical transfer functions.
The model is tested on numerous theoretical transfer functions, and shows promising improvements when
compared to full-scale derived transfer functions.
Keywords:
Container ship, Sea keeping, full-scale, HydroSTAR, transfer functions, uncertainty

Preface
This report covers the thesis work conducted by the author as partial fulﬁlment of the requirement for
obtaining a Master degree in Engineering Design and Applied Mechanics at the Department of Mechanical
Engineering at The Technical University of Denmark (DTU).
The presented work was carried out in the period between September 2014 and March 2015 at the Section
of Fluid Mechanics, Coastal and Marine Engineering, Department of Mechanical Engineering at DTU in
Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark, and has been supervised by Associate Professor Ulrik Dam Nielsen. The work
load corresponds to 30 points in the European Credit Transfer System (ECTS).
It is believed by the author that the present report will provide a good insight into the methods used for
determining full-scale transfer function for a large container ship in addition to evaluate the accuracy of
theoretical numerical computed transfer functions.
The author would like to use the opportunity to personally thank a number of people who all have been
very helpful in the project period:
- To my supervisor, Associate Professor Ulrik Dam Nielsen for his help and guidance throughout this
project, and taking his time to fully discuss the various assumptions, problems and results on our weekly
meeting. An extra thanks should be given for quickly replying to my emails no matter time or day.
- To PhD student Najmeh Montazeri for computing and providing me with Vertical Bending Moment
transfer functions.
- To my family and friends for fully supporting me and for giving me time and space to write this thesis.
- To my beautiful girlfriend for her moral support and extremely large tolerance to my varying mental
presence and many late nights spend at the oﬃce in the late stage of this project.
Kongens Lyngby, Denmark, March 2015

Chapter 1
Introduction
In recent years there has been increasing focus on the application of onboard, real-time operator guidance
for ships in terms of decision support systems [Nielsen, 2010]. These decision support systems are often
an integrated part of an onboard system that monitors and records the data or response of interest. This
could typically be a system that provides navigational guidance to the master of the ship, with the overall
goal of providing advices to course and/or speed changes relative to the waves. This guidance is basically
due to a concern for non-desired eﬀects which could typically include the following:
• Damage to cargo due to large accelerations
• Shift or loss of cargo
• Hull girder damage
• fatigue damage accumulation in critical structural details
All of these non-desired eﬀects are often associated to speciﬁc ship responses. The methodology for
predicting these responses is a mathematical/hydrodynamical model of the ship behaviour in waves
coupled with an on-site wave energy distribution. This mathematical model of a ships behaviour in
waves are governed by complex transfer functions and assumption of linearity between the waves and the
associated responses. The better the mathematical models and the more accurate the wave estimation,
the better the prediction of ship responses. Better prediction of ship responses leads to better prediction
of all the non-desired eﬀects listed above, which again leads to better decision support systems.
Improving these mathematical models, or transfer functions, by use of full-scale measurements and
uncertainty modelling are the focus point of this project.
1
==PAGE:14==
1.1. LITERATURE SURVEY
1.1
Literature Survey
To establish a foundation in knowledge on the area of full-scale measurements on a ship, decision support
systems, transfer functions, uncertainty modelling and general sea-keeping, a literature study was carried
out. This section presents the main ﬁndings.
[Nielsen, 2010] summarises the importance and interest in decision support systems. He describes how
these systems can be used to giving decision support to the ship’s master with respect to speed and/or
course changes, so that any non-desired eﬀects are avoided. These non-desired eﬀects could include among
others: seasickness of passengers, damage to cargo due to large accelerations, shift or loss of cargo due
to a combination of roll and acceleration, etc. He also describes how such systems methodology for the
prediction of future responses rely on theoretical calculated transfer functions.
[Soares, 1991] presents a study for quantifying model uncertainty factors for a set of theoretical models
used for calculating transfer functions. The paper suggests how these uncertainty factors can be used to
correct the predictions of theoretical models. The study uses theoretical calculated transfer functions and
transfer functions obtained through experiments to determined model uncertainty factors.
A study by [Davis et al., 2005] investigated how full-scale transfer functions could be determined by use
of radar observations of encounter wave proﬁle and measured vessel motions for a high-speed catamaran.
The obtained full-scale transfer functions were also compared to theoretical calculated transfer functions.
Another study by [Jacobi et al., 2012] also assess the behaviour of a large high-speed craft. This was
done by extensive full-scale trials conducted by the U.S. Navy. This was done by systematic for dif-
ferent speeds, sea states and diﬀerent headings. The collected data was used to determine full-scale
transfer functions to characterise the ships sea-keeping performance of the catamaran. The full-scale trans-
fer functions was, as the study by [Davis et al., 2005], compared to theoretical calculated transfer functions.
Finally, although all of the studies mentioned in this literature list have touched upon the subject of
either full-scale determination of transfer functions or modelling uncertainties, none of the studies have
combined the use of uncertainty modelling to improve theoretical transfer functions for potential use in
decision support systems. The author thus ﬁnds it reasonable to dedicate this study to investigating this
subject more in details.
2
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.2
Problem Formulation and Objectives
This project will determine the transfer functions of a large container ship by use of recorded full-scale data.
The transfer functions will be determined for heave, roll and pitch motion in addition to vertical bending
moment and vertical acceleration. This is possible since the wave spectrum is continuously measured
by wave radar(s). These derived full-scale transfer functions will be compared to theoretical calculated
transfer functions from third party. If variations are found between full-scale and theoretical transfer
functions, a model for quantifying and adjusting the theoretical transfer function uncertainties is suggested.
The objectives can be summarized to be:
• Determination of transfer functions via full-scale measurements.
• Compare derived full-scale transfer functions to theoretically calculated transfer functions.
• Adjust theoretical calculated transfer functions to better ﬁt full-scale transfer functions via uncertainty
modelling.
1.3
Outline of the Report
The report is organized the following way:
• Chapter 2 Introduces the notation, studied vessel and full-scale data used throughout this project.
The method used to identify stationary time periods in full-scale data is presented together with the
located stationary time periods.
• Chapter 3 presents the theory for determining transfer functions from full-scale measurements
together with the obtained results.
• Chapter 4 covers the comparison of full-scale transfer functions, obtained in chapter 3, with theoretical
transfer functions numerically computed by the classiﬁcation company Bureau Veritas’ by use of
in-house software HydroSTAR.
• Chapter 5 quantiﬁes the uncertainty between the full-scale and HydroSTAR transfer functions.
A model for adjusting the theoretical calculated transfer functions to better ﬁt full-scale transfer
functions is outlined.
• Chapter 6 presents the results from applying the model from chapter 5 to HydroSTAR transfer
functions.
• Chapter 7 discuss the results and underlying assumptions.
• Chapter 8 summarizes the work and ﬁndings and outlines the most important conclusion. A
suggestion for further work is also presented.
3
==PAGE:16==

Chapter 2
Studied Vessel and Full-Scale Data
Chapter two presents the data used for all analysis throughout this report. In late 2006 the CMA-CGM
container vessel Rigoletto was equipped with an extensive instrumentation system for a joint industry
project named Lashing@Sea. The Lashing@Sea project was focussing on the loads imposed to the cargo
and the system performed very well collecting a large quantity of high quality data until the end of the
project in 2009. In 2010, a new project called Tools for Ultra Large Container Ships (TULCS) set out
using the same data collecting system as the Lashing@Sea while adding additional sensors focussing
speciﬁcally on the loads induced in the hull. The data used in this report originates from the TULCS
project ([CD, 2012],[Koning, 2010] and [Fresser, 2010]).
The TULCS project data can be divided into three overall categories: navigation, wave and response data.
Navigation data includes position, speed, heading and wind information. Wave data contains information
regarding sea state in terms of radar scatter photos of the sea surface. Measured ship responses such as
heave, roll, pitch and vertical bending moment time histories are included in response data.
Figure 2.1: CMA-CGM Rigoletto.[Koning, 2010]
Figure 2.2: Global position of measurements: 1)
August 12th, 2) August 20th, 3) September 16th,
4) September 20th, 5) October 2nd.
The data are collected while the ship traded on a route between Asia and Northern Europe. Five 24-hour
measurement periods have been picked out from a couple of years of measurements. The ﬁve data sets
were chosen based on criteria such as: all measurement sensors being on-line simultaneously and the
ship being operated at relatively constant navigation conditions. The ﬁve periods are all recorded during
2011 and the position of the ship on days of measurements can be seen on ﬁgure 2.2. The ﬁrst data set
is collected on August 12th in the sea between the Golf of Aden and the Arabian Sea with a draft of
D = 14.2m. The second data set is collected in the Mediterranean Sea near Gibraltar on August 16th
5
==PAGE:18==
with a draft of D = 14.2m. On September 16th the third data set were collected in the Golf of Aden
with a draft of D = 14.0m. The forth data set was collected in the Indian Ocean on September 20th with
a draft of D = 14.0m. The last and ﬁfth data set is collected on October 2nd in the South China Sea
sailing with a draft of D = 15m.
The studied vessel named CMA-CGM Rigoletto is a large container vessel build on Hyundai Heavy
Industries in South Korea in 2006. The vessel is owned and operated by the French shipping company
CMA-CGM. The vessel is 350 meters long, 42.8 meters wide with a maximum draught of 14.5 meters.
The engine is a MAN Diesel with roughly 69 MW with can power the vessel to a maximum speed of 25.4
knots. The maximum capacity of the vessel is 9,415 TEU and 108,000 tons deadweight. An overview of
the main particulars can be seen in table 2.1.

CHAPTER 2. STUDIED VESSEL AND FULL-SCALE DATA
2.1
Conventions and Notations
Before elaborating further on data or analysis of data, the convention and notation used in this thesis
will be established. Conventions and notations used throughout this report follows to a large degree the
notation used in the TULCS project documentation [CD, 2012], [Koning, 2010] and [Fresser, 2010].
2.1.1
Ship Motions and Coordinates
Any particular ship’s motion time history can be represented by a combination of the time histories of three
linear and three rotational displacements. These six displacements are deﬁned using the right-handed axis
system shown on ﬁgure 2.3. The position of the ships center of gravity is deﬁned by the three linear dis-
placements: surge, sway and heave which are denoted r1, r2 and r3 respectively. The attitude of the ship is
deﬁned by three angular displacements: roll, pitch and yaw which are denoted by r4, r5 and r6 respectively.
The majority of traditional ships have port/starboard symmetry which makes surge, heave and pitch
so-called symmetric motions due to being placed in the plane of symmetry. Sway, roll and yaw are called
antisymmetric motions. However, in this project motions have been recorded in starboard side which
means that the vertical motion data provided is not heave motion only, but includes a contribution from
pitch and roll. To keep the same notation used throughout the TULCS project, the vertical motion is
denoted heave throughout this thesis, even though pitch and roll are coupled to the motion.
Heave
Surge
Sway
Pitch
Roll
Yaw
Figure 2.3: Ship motions
2.1.2
Headings
In this thesis three diﬀerent headings are worked with: mean vessel heading µs, mean wave heading µw
and relative mean wave heading µ. Vessel heading µs is measured by GPS and is given in traditional
compass rose style with 0 degree being North, 90 degree being East, 180 degree being South and 270
degree being West. The mean wave heading µw is measured using the WaMoS radar system (see section
2.3) and is given relative to north as the vessel heading. The relative mean wave heading µ is the angle
between the sailing direction of the vessel and the mean wave heading. The relative mean wave heading
can be calculated when both mean vessel heading and mean wave heading are known. The relative mean
wave heading is 0 degree when waves and vessel is moving in the same direction and 180 degree when
moving in opposite direction. The deﬁnitions can be seen on ﬁgure 2.4. The ﬁgure shows that a relative
mean wave heading in the range 0 to 30 degree is denoted following waves, 30 to 60 degree range is denoted
quartering waves, 60 to 120 degree range is denoted beam waves, 120 to 150 range degree is denoted bow
7
==PAGE:20==
2.1. CONVENTIONS AND NOTATIONS
waves and 150 to 180 degree range is denoted head waves. The relative mean wave heading will in the
remaining report be denoted just relative wave heading without loss of generality.

2.1.3
Ocean Waves
Figure 2.5 shows part of a typical record of wave elevation in the ocean (can be obtained for example by a
wave sensing devise or wave radar). The ﬁgure shows an irregular nature and no consistent pattern can
easily be observed. The characteristics of the irregular ocean waves can be quantiﬁed by use of statistical
methods [Lloyd, 1989, pp.94]. Two basic measurements will be used in this thesis to describe the ocean
waves: wave heigh H and wave zero-upcrossing period Tz. The wave height is the vertical distance from
a peak to a succeeding trough and the wave zero-upcrossing period is the time between two successive
upward zero crossings as seen on ﬁgure 2.5. These individual measurements are unique to the particular
part of the record chosen for analysis and therefore are of little use for characterising the whole time
history. Instead mean values of these quantities are used to describe the general characteristics of the
complete time history. ¯Tz denotes the mean value of many measurements of Tz.
¯
Hs denotes the mean
value of the highest third of wave height measurements, which is often used instead of mean value of all
wave height measurements. These mean values can be determined by analysis of a wave record in the
time domain or by spectral analysis in the frequency domain.

H
⇣(m)
t(s)
Figure 2.5: Typical wave record. Analysis of peaks and troughs.

CHAPTER 2. STUDIED VESSEL AND FULL-SCALE DATA
2.2
Navigation data
All relevant navigation data such as the vessels position, heading and speed are constantly sampled from
the Electronic Chart Display and Information System (ECDIS) on the bridge on Rigoletto and saved
together with a time stamp in a data ﬁle on a data logging PC.
2.3
Wave data
The sea state measurement system installed on board the Rigoletto is composed of two diﬀerent systems:
a Wave Monitoring System (WaMoS) lighting the surroundings from the top of the wheelhouse and a
Wave Guide system (RADAC) looking down at the bow. The position of the two system installations can
be seen on ﬁgure 2.6a. The WaMoS system is a X-band radar which scans the surrounding sea surface.
The system uses raw radar images to extract sea clutter from where the surface elevation and sea state
parameters are computed (ﬁgure 2.6b). The sea state parameters are identiﬁed for a JONSWAP spectrum.
In addition, a frequency-direction spectrum is obtained from surface elevation.
The RADAC system is a X-band radar, which measures the sea surface elevation at the bow of the ship.
A radar is mounted on both port- and starboard side named WGS and WOB respectively. The radars are
equipped with inertial measurement units to compensate the ship motions.
The quality of the estimated sea state depends strongly on the conditions in which the ship operates (wind,
rain, etc). For example, for WaMoS system the backscatter intensity depends on the surface elevation
as well as the sea surface roughness (i.e. no ripples at the sea surface reduces the backscatter intensity).
The RADAC measurements are potentially aﬀected by refracted waves and/or sprays at the bow. In this
project, the sea state is predicted using the WaMos system.
(a) Position of systems
(b) WaMoS system principle
Figure 2.6: Sea state measurement. source: [CD, 2012]
2.4
Response data
Sway, heave, roll and pitch motion data is measured using six degree of freedom accelerometers positioned
in the starboard side of the vessel. Only heave, roll and pitch motion are considered in this project.
Stress measurements are measured amidship and converted into vertical bending moment (VBM) at the
centreline. The vertical bending moment have been low-pass ﬁltered for higher frequencies to neglect
hydro-elastic eﬀects.
2.5
System overview and data ﬁles
Figure 2.7 shows the complete overview of the data sampling system installed on board Rigoletto. The
ﬁgure shows a very comprehensive collection of data from the numerous sensors and measuring devices.
It should be noted that data from slamming is omitted in this project. The RADAC wave altimetry
measurements are, together with measured ship motions, send through the ships network to the data
logging computed in the ships engine control room (ECR). Data from the WaMoS system is stored on a
9
==PAGE:22==
2.6. STATIONARY TIME PERIODS
local PC in the wheelhouse, which is able to forward data through the ships network to the ECR-PC for
further processing and storage. Navigation systems are connected to the WaMoS PC in the wheelhouse
which also collect and stores navigation data, due to WaMoS processing software requiring various
navigation parameters such as speed, heading, etc.
Figure 2.7: System diagram
Two diﬀerent types of data ﬁles are extracted from logged data and provides as foundation for calculations
in this report. The ﬁrst type of dataﬁles are containing WaMoS and navigation data while the secoond
type of dataﬁles contains RADAC wave altimetry measurements and ship motions. WaMoS and navigation
data are calculated as mean values over a 20 minutes period and stored every 5 minutes in a combined
data ﬁle for each time period. An example of such data ﬁle can be seen in appendix A(appendix A is
limited to the ﬁrst four pages of the data ﬁle). RADAC wave altimetry measurements and ship motions
are sampled in a rate of 20 Hz and stored in a data ﬁle for each of the ﬁve days of measurements with a

2.6
Stationary Time Periods
All provided full-scale data is measured continuously over 24 hour periods. Variations in sea state or
operation of the ship is very likely to happen when doing measurements of that time scale. Changes in
both sea state or operation yields additional uncertainties in analysis and it is thus of high importance to
1The provided data for this project has been converted to a 5Hz sampling rate.
10
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2. STUDIED VESSEL AND FULL-SCALE DATA
locate stationary time periods. A time period is said to be stationary when measured parameters are
independent of time. Stationary ocean waves and derived load processes may typically have a duration of
20 minutes to 3 hours [Nielsen, 2010]. Time periods of 20 minutes is convenient to use in this project,
since WaMoS and navigational data are averaged over a 20 minutes period.
To identify the 20 minutes stationary time periods within the ﬁve days of data, some analysis is to be
carried out. The starting point in this analysis is the sea state and navigation data. In order to have
a stationary time period, the signiﬁcant wave hight Hs, wave zero-upcrossing period Tz, relative wave
heading µ and ship speed V would have to be time independent. Ship speed, ship and wave heading can
be read directly from WaMoS II data ﬁles for a given time period2, while the signiﬁcant wave height and
zero-upcrossing period can be calculated from a recorded wave energy spectrum.

The total energy in a wave spectrum is constant regardless of the frequency unit being Hertz or radians
per second. The area under the wave spectrum within a small wave frequency interval δf is proportional
to the energy contained within that band of frequencies. Transforming the spectrum from Hertz to radians
per second does not change this energy, and the area within the wave frequency range δ! must be identical
to the corresponding frequency range δf (ﬁgure 2.9). The ordinates of the wave spectrum is therefore

A method for determining all relevant parameters that are required to be stationary, has now been
established. The next step is to check how the various parameters change over time and locate stationary
time periods. In order to assume a time period i stationary, the considered parameters (Hs, Tz, V, D, µ)
would have to also be identical to both the parameters in previous time period (i −1) and the parameters
in the following time period (i + 1) (ﬁgure 2.11). To determine which time periods are stationary, the
mean, standard deviation and CoV3 of the parameters (Hs, Tz, V, D, µ) are calculated over the three
consecutive time periods, and then stored to time period i. The time period is then shifted one data
ﬁle forward, and mean and standard deviation are computed again, and stored to corresponding time
period. This process is repeated until the mean and standard deviation has been computed for every three
consecutive 20 minute time periods and stored to the middle time period.

minutes of measurements.

12th (time histories for other days can be seen in appendix B). Based on the data on ﬁgure 2.12, the time
periods with lowest standard deviation or CoV are selected. These time periods are more likely to have
stationary sea state and operation parameters.

August 12th is seen to be a relatively calm day with vessel speeds of 22.6 and 23.0 knot and relative mean

24-25 knot range and relative wave headings between 141 degree and 153 degree, which are categorised as
bow/head waves. September 16th shows moderate sea state with signiﬁcant wave heights up to 3.3 meters.

Chapter 3
Determination of Transfer Functions Using
Full-Scale Measurements
This chapter covers the determination of transfer functions, using full-scale measurements. The background
for the various full-scale measurements used in this chapter is explained in chapter one.
The assessment of seakeeping performance in irregular waves can be made by an analogy to the electronics
and communications ﬁelds. Although this method were developed in 1953 they it still among the most
used for assessment of seakeeping performance [Lloyd, 1989].
The analogy suggested that the ship could be treated as a black box electronic ﬁlter. The black box
electronic ﬁlter works by receiving an input signal containing a number of diﬀerent frequency components
and amplifying or weaken the signal to a modiﬁed output signal according to the characteristics of the
ﬁlter. The ships black box analogy suggest that the ship can be seen as a ﬁlter of waves instead of
electrical signals (ﬁgure 3.1). In fact, the ship ﬁlter should not be considered a single ﬁlter. Instead, the
ship should be considered as a collection of ﬁlters, each with its own individual characteristics. This is
due to diﬀerent ﬁlter characteristics for diﬀerent load conditions, vessel speeds and relative wave headings.
This collection of ﬁlters is commonly denoted transfer functions, due to the ability of transferring wave
amplitudes into motion amplitudes. It is assumed that the ﬁlter is linear, i.e. the output signal amplitude
(ship motion) at any given frequency is linearly proportional to the input signal amplitude (wave).
Filter
Input
Output
Ship
Waves
Motions
Figure 3.1: The electronic ﬁlter analogy. [Nielsen, 2010]
This concept can be formalised and quantiﬁed by use of so-called spectral analysis. Spectral analysis
is mathematically valid and rigorous if ship motion responses are linear to the wave amplitude at any
given speed, heading and frequency. This is almost always true as long as the motions and waves are of
small amplitude [Nielsen, 2010, pp.57]. For a given known encounter wave energy spectrum the vessels
motion energy spectrum - the so-called response spectrum - can be determined for the individual motion
17
==PAGE:30==
3.1. ENCOUNTER WAVE SPECTRUM
component by ﬁltering the encountered wave energy spectrum with the appropriate ﬁlter or transfer
function. The ﬁltering of the encounter wave energy spectrum is performed by multiplying each ordinate
of the encountered wave spectrum by the square of the amplitude of the motion transfer function at the
corresponding encounter frequency i.e.:
Sri(!e) = S⇣(!e)
✓ri,0
⇣0
◆2
(3.0.1)
where Sri(!e) is the response spectrum, S⇣(!e) is encountered wave spectrum and
⇣
ri,0
⇣0
⌘
is the non-
dimensionalized amplitude function of the motion transfer function.
The transfer functions for a ship are found by solving the equations of motions for the particular ship.
The equations can be solved by use of various tools and may include experimental or numerical meth-
ods. Typically transfer functions for a given motion consist of motion amplitude functions and phase
angles. The amplitude function, also called response amplitude operator (RAO), ri,0 give the propor-
tion of wave amplitude transferred by the ship into motions, where the phase angle δi give the phase
relationship between the motion and the wave. In this project, amplitudes are of interest and phase
angles are ignored, i.e. use of the term transfer functions are referring to amplitude functions in this project.
Determining the transfer functions for a full-scale container ship from full-scale measurements are of
interest. This can be done by use of equation 3.0.2. Instead of using the transfer functions and wave
spectrum to predict motions, transfer functions can be computed if both wave spectrum and response
spectrum are known. The transfer function can therefore be determined as:
ri,0
⇣0

S⇣(!e)
(3.0.2)
When considering the eﬀects of waves on the angular motions of ships (pitch, roll) it is often convenient
to express the energy of the wave system in terms of a wave slope spectrum instead of the conventional
wave energy spectrum (also called wave amplitude spectrum). The transfer functions for angular motions
are thus determined as:

(3.0.4)
3.1
Encounter Wave spectrum
The determination of wave spectra was examined in chapter one. The wave spectra was found by use
of WaMoS radar system, which utilized radar images to extract sea state data. The radar images was
converted into directional wave spectra, which could be integrated to obtain one-dimensional wave spectra.
The obtained wave energy spectra was given as a function of wave frequency !. Although the wave
frequency ! has some inﬂuence on ship motions, the motions depend more on the frequency of which the
moving ship encounters the waves (i.e. encounter frequency !e). For instance, in head and bow waves the
ship is heading into the waves and would therefore experience encounter frequencies larger than the wave
frequencies. In other words, the wave spectrum needs to be transformed from an observed wave spectrum
at a ﬁxed point in the ocean to an observed wave spectrum which follows the reference frame of a moving
18
==PAGE:31==

As previously seen in ﬁgure 2.9, transforming the wave spectrum from frequency in Hz to rad/s did not
change the total energy in the spectrum. The same is valid when transforming the spectrum from wave
frequency to encounter frequency. Transforming the spectrum to the moving frame of reference of the ship
does not change the energy within the spectrum, and it follows that the area within the wave frequency
range δ! must be exactly reproduced as an equal area within the corresponding encounter frequency range
δ!e. Thus, the ordinates of the wave spectrum and the encounter wave spectrum must be related by:

3.2. RESPONSE SPECTRUM
3.2
Response spectrum
As stated in chapter one, data ﬁles containing continuous measured time histories for all responses are
provided. Such time history, ﬁltered to show only time period 246 on August 12th are shown on ﬁgure
3.3. Spectral analysis has the frequency domain as its model space where analysis of ship responses
conveniently can be performed, as long as the linear assumption between waves and ship holds (since
a ships behaviour is completely described by its transfer functions). The frequency domain is therefore
appreciated when performing calculations used for assessment of ship responses, although it is not a
physically observable domain as the time domain (i.e. time history).
Due to the stochastic behaviour of a time history only a few statistical measures, such as the mean value
and variance, can be used for comparison. The frequency domain reveal characteristics of a higher degree
of details such as the possibility of comparing the ordinates of the derived spectrum at each frequency
in addition to any derived statistical measure. Thus, the frequency domain is often preferred and it is
therefore of great interest to transform the domain from the actual physical domain - the time domain -
to the frequency domain.
Fast Fourier Transformation (FFT) is a method often used to transform from the time domain to the
frequency domain. The FFT method oﬀers an easily applicable tool for calculation of response spectral
densities from measured time histories. According to [Penny, 2000], direct application of FFT leads to a
high variance estimate of the power at a given frequency, which means that estimated spectra will be very
spiky. This high variance estimate of the power reduces accuracy and reliability of results which depends
on spectra obtained by use of FFT. A method for compensating for this phenomena involve segmentation
with possible overlap of the time series and the use of window functions1. A toolbox for Matlab named
WAFO2 is used to apply this Fast Fourier Transformation method.

3.2.1
WAFO
WAFO is a toolbox for Matlab which includes various routines for statistical analysis and simulation of
random waves and random loads. In this thesis, WAFO will be used to transform the response signal
from time domain to frequency domain. WAFO can apply the window functions needed to reduce the
spikes of the obtained spectra, but the window size will have to be determined manually. According to
[WAFO, 2011] a time signal can be transformed from time domain to frequency domain by use of following
Matlab syntax:
1
S = dat2spec(x,L)
where x is a data matrix with sampled times in the ﬁrst column and values in the next columns, and L is
the window size. Figure 3.4 shows the results from applying WAFO to the time histories from ﬁgure 3.3.
The ﬁgure shows the obtained spectra from raw FFT and by applying a window size of L = 400. The plot
clearly shows how the spectra is smoothed by appliance of L value. It should also be realised that by
using a smaller window size, the spectrum would even smoother - but also that smaller window size will
result in loss of information. For now, an L value of 400 will be used for computing response spectra. A
discussion on optimal L value is done in the discussion and results chapter.

3.2.2
Vertical acceleration
Data have been provided only for heave, roll, pitch and vertical bending moment.
Often, vertical
accelerations are a response which is looked into, especially when it comes to dimensioning of the lashing
systems on container ships. This section presents how the vertical acceleration response spectrum can be
derived relatively easily from measured heave data.
Two diﬀerent methods can be used to derive the vertical acceleration response spectrum: double diﬀeren-
tiation in either the time domain or in frequency domain.
21
==PAGE:34==

3.3. TRANSFER FUNCTIONS
3.3
Transfer functions
From the derived wave spectra and response spectra covered in the last sections, the response amplitude
operators or RAO’s can easily be computed by equation 3.0.2 for linear responses and from equation
3.0.3 for angular responses. The derived wave spectra and response spectra, together with the associated
RAO’s, are presented in the following sections. Six set of data have been selected from all time periods, to
give a general view of the results. The chosen data sets are following: time period 246 from August 12th,
time period 15 from August 20th, time period 214 from September 16th, time period 67 from September
20th, time period 182 from September 20th and time period 101 from October 2nd. The selected data
sets are chosen to include a variety of vessel speeds, relative wave headings and drafts.
The heave motion response amplitude operators can be seen on ﬁgure 3.8. A general observation is that all
the obtained RAO’s increase as frequency decrease below approximately 0.4 rad/s. This behaviour is not as
expected. The heave motion RAO’s is expected to approach unity as frequency moves towards zero. This is
due to the long waves connected with low frequencies. For very long waves, the ships vertical motion simply
follows the surface elevation ([Lloyd, 1989, pp.236]). Figure 3.8a and 3.8b shows clearly why this low
frequency problem occurs. The response spectra on both ﬁgures (the bottom spectra) shows a presence of
energy at lower frequencies, while no energy is present in the same low frequency range in the wave energy
spectra. This leads to RAO values of inﬁnite magnitude. The fact that energy is present at the heave
response spectrum in frequency ranges that contains almost no energy in the wave energy spectrum, could
indicate that the spectra obtained by the WaMoS radar is not 100 percent correct for lower frequencies. Be-
cause of this low frequency problem, the obtained response amplitude operators are considered to be correct
only within a certain frequency range. This frequency range would have to be assessed in each isolated case.
The obtained heave motion RAO for time period 15 on August 20th seen on ﬁgure 3.8b seems incorrect
compared to the RAO’s obtained from other time periods. The low value ordinates of the wave energy
spectrum and response spectrum indicates very little energy in the wave system, which could indicate that
weather conditions are simply to mild for predicting the Heave motion RAO. The same problem can be
observed for vertical bending moment and vertical acceleration RAO’s obtained on the August 20th (ﬁgure
3.11b and 3.12b). The rotational displacements (pitch and roll) seems to be untroubled by the mild weather,
as ordinates on wave slope and response spectra on ﬁgure 3.9b and 3.10b both are within the same range as
ordinates on other time periods. All transfer functions obtained for heave, vertical bending moment and ver-
tical acceleration on August 20th are thus considered incorrect, and will be omitted for the remaining study.
In general, the obtained full-scale heave motion RAO’s show peak values at approximately !e = 0.6 radians
per second with amplitudes varying depending on which speed, draft and relative wave heading they are
derived at. The derived heave motion transfer functions are considered to be valid in the frequency range
!e = 0.4 to 2 radians per seconds.
The derived roll motion RAO’s are seen on ﬁgure 3.9. The ﬁgure shows relatively consistent results for all
time periods with small variations in steepness. The RAO’s are seen to be deﬁned in a narrower frequency
range compared to the heave motion RAO’s. The derived roll motion RAO’s are considered correct within
the approximate range !e = 0.5 to 2 radians per second.
Figure 3.10 shows the derived full-scale pitch motion RAO’s. The derived pitch motion transfer functions
are relative consistent in terms of shape, with small variation in steepness. The transfer functions are
considered valid in the frequency range !e = 0.5 to 2 radians per second.
24
==PAGE:37==
CHAPTER 3. DETERMINATION OF TRANSFER FUNCTIONS USING FULL-SCALE
MEASUREMENTS
Derived vertical bending moment RAO’s are shown on ﬁgure 3.11. The ﬁgure shows a large variation in
peak frequency and amplitude depending on draft, speed and relative wave heading. The largest peak
amplitude is seen for time period 246 on August 12th with V = 21.1 and µ = 118◦. The amplitude is
approximately 0.9 times the wave amplitude and occurring at frequency 0.4-0.5 radians per second. The
RAO’s obtained for time periods with beam waves (ﬁgure 3.11c, 3.11d and 3.11e) are seen to have a more
uniform distribution of amplitudes, compared to the two time periods with larger relative wave head-
ings. The derived VBM RAO’s are considered valid in the frequency range !e = 0.4 to 2 radians per second.
Vertical acceleration RAO’s are shown on ﬁgure 3.12. The ﬁgure shows peak at approximately !e = 0.6
radians per second for all of the obtained RAO’s with amplitudes ranging from !e = 0.9 to 1.4 depending
on draft, speed and relative wave heading. The vertical acceleration RAO’s are considered to be valid
from !e = 0.4 to 2.0 radians per second.

Chapter 4
Comparison of Full-Scale Derived and
Theoretical Transfer Functions
Transfer functions were derived from full-scale measurements in chapter 3 for the following responses: heave,
roll, pitch, vertical bending moment and vertical acceleration. In this chapter, a comparison will be made
between the full-scale derived transfer functions and theoretical calculated transfer functions made by the
classiﬁcation company Bureau Veritas (BV)1. The theoretical calculated transfer functions are computed
by BV using the numerical in-house software HydroSTAR. HydroSTAR is a hydrodynamic sea-keeping
software developed by BV to evaluate wave loads and induced motions on ships2. All theoretical transfer
functions are calculated at the same position as the response motions are measured. This makes full-scale
and theoretical transfer functions directly comparable.
4.1
Interpolation
Determining transfer functions by numerical methods are often computational heavy, and calculations will
have to be performed for a variety of conditions due to the transfer functions dependency of draft, heading
and speed, as mentioned earlier. Theoretical determined transfer functions are thus always computed for
a discrete number of drafts, relative wave headings and vessel speeds.
The RAO’s computed by BV, using HydroSTAR, are given for angles of 10◦from 0◦to 360◦and for
vessel speeds ranging from 5 knot to 25 knot by step of 5 knot and three loading conditions: D = 10.5m,
D = 12.5m and D = 14.5m3. In addition, the RAO’s are given in the frequency interval 0.05 radians per
second to 2 radians per second in 0.05 radians per second steps, i.e. the RAO’s are given for 30 frequencies
for each draft, heading and speed combination. Examples of the numerical computed RAO’s can be seen
on ﬁgure 4.1 for heave and pitch motion at head, bow and beam waves (V = 20kn, D = 14m). Figure 4.1a
shows that heave motions peak are expected for head waves at approximately 0.4 radians per second with
an amplitude of 2.5. When the relative wave heading changes, the peak changes to wave frequency 0.5
radians per second with an amplitude of 2.4 for bow waves and 0.6 radians per second with an amplitude
of 1.2 for beam waves respectively. Examples of numerical computed RAO’s can be seen on ﬁgure 4.1b for
pitch. The ﬁgure shows that the largest pitch motions are expected for head waves. The peak value is
found at wave frequency 0.3 radians per second with a magnitude of one. When the vessel is experiencing
bow waves, the peak amplitude is reduced to 0.5 at 0.4 radians per second. At beam waves, the peak
1transfer functions for vertical bending moment are computed by DTU PhD student Najmeh Montazeri using I-Ship
software. The I-Ship software is based on linear strip theory.
2www.veristar.com
3transfer functions computed by I-Ship follows the same distrete structure.
31
==PAGE:44==

value is reduced to approximately 0.05 in magnitude and the wave frequency is increased to 0.6 radians
per second.

Figure 4.1: Numerical transfer functions computed by HydroSTAR.
When comparing transfer functions extracted from full-scale measurements to discrete theoretical calculated
transfer functions, the foundation for comparison is often not ideal. The full-scale measurements are most
likely collected at a random draft with a random relative wave heading at a random vessel speed, while
theoretical calculated transfer functions are computed for a number of discrete conditions. The transfer
functions are thus not directly comparable. Figure 4.2 shows for which relative headings and vessel speeds
the transfer functions are computed for both full-scale and theoretical.

calculated.
The ﬁgure shows that all of the full-scale transfer functions are obtained at velocities and relative wave
headings that lies between values at which the discrete HydroSTAR transfer functions are computed.
Due to this oﬀset, the HydroSTAR transfer functions have to be interpolated to ﬁt the velocities and
relative wave headings of the full-scale RAO’s. The RAO’s are interpolated between both velocity and
relative wave heading4. Figure 4.3 shows how a given RAO is interpolated between four RAO’s. As an
example, if RAO(V, µ) denotes the HydroSTAR RAO computed at speed V and relative wave heading µ.
Then, for a full-scale RAO obtained from measurements collected at a vessel speed of V = 17kn with a
relative wave heading of µ = 103, the comparable HydroSTAR RAO is found as: 1) interpolation between
4The transfer functions are not interpolated between draft, instead transfer functions for 14.5 meters draft are used in
all calculations. This is the case for RAO’s from both HydroSTAR and I-ship.

RAO(15kn, 100◦) and RAO(15kn, 110◦) to obtain RAO(15kn, 103◦), 2) interpolation of RAO(20kn, 100◦)
and RAO(20kn, 110◦) to obtained RAO(20kn, 103◦), 3) interpolation between velocities by interpolate
between RAO(10kn, 103◦) and RAO(20kn, 103◦) to obtained RAO(17kn, 103◦).

to ﬁt the vessel speed and relative wave heading of the full-scale transfer functions.

4.2. HEAVE
4.2
Heave
Figure 4.4a shows transfer functions at V = 23.1kn, D = 14.2m and µ = 105◦. Peaks are seen to be at
approximately !e = 0.6 with amplitudes of 2.2 and 1.7 for full-scale and HydroSTAR respectively. The
RAOs on ﬁgure 4.4b conﬁrm that the full-scale heave motion RAO obtained on August 20th is unﬁt for
use, when compared to HydroSTAR RAO. The RAOs computed for time period 214 on September 16th
(ﬁgure 4.4c) show that HydroSTAR correctly predicts the peak frequency to !e = 0.6 and peak amplitude
to 1.6, but overestimate the amplitudes at lower encounter frequencies. On ﬁgure 4.4d HydroSTAR is seen
to overestimate the amplitudes in the encounter frequency range !e = 0.4 to 0.5 and underestimate the
amplitudes for encounter frequencies above 0.5. For time period 182 on September 20th, HydroSTAR is
seen to predict both the amplitudes and peak frequency quite accurate, except for frequencies in the range
!e = 0.4 to 0.5 which are overestimated slightly. Figure 4.4f shows a large diﬀerence in amplitudes in the
encounter frequency range !e = 0.4 to 0.7, where HydroSTAR overestimate by approximately 45 percent.

4.3
Roll

4.4. PITCH
4.4
Pitch
Compared to the roll motion RAOs on ﬁgure 4.5, pitch motion RAOs on ﬁgure 4.6 shows more resemblance
between the full-scale and HydroSTAR results. Based on the wave energy and response spectra from
ﬁgure 3.10, the full-scale RAOs are considered to be valid in the encounter frequency range from !e = 0.5
to 2.0.
Based on this, the HydroSTAR RAOs on ﬁgure 4.6a, 4.6b, 4.6c and 4.6d all seems to be
underestimated in the frequency range !e = 0.5 to 0.7 compared to full-scale RAOs. Figure 4.6f shows
great resemblance between full-scale and HydroSTAR for headwaves, while ﬁgure 4.6d shows a signiﬁcant
amplitude underestimate by HydroSTAR for all frequencies at relative wave heading µ = 91.

4.6. VERTICAL ACCELERATION
4.6
Vertical Acceleration
Figure 4.8 shows the comparison of full-scale and HydroSTAR vertical acceleration RAO’s derived for
all the studied time periods. Figure 4.8a shows that the peak predicted by HydroSTAR is at !e = 0.75
while full-scale show actual peak at 0.6. HydroSTAR also underestimate the amplitudes at frequencies
below this peak frequency, and overestimate amplitude for frequencies above. On ﬁgure 4.8c, 4.8e and
4.8f, the peak frequencies at HydroSTAR RAOs are equal to the peak frequencies of the full-scale RAOs
at !e = 0.7, 0.7 and 0.6 respectively. The amplitude is underestimated at frequencies below !e = 0.85 and
overestimate for frequencies above. Figure 4.8d shows a large underestimate in amplitude by HydroSTAR
in the frequency range !e = 0.4 to 1.0 for relative wave heading µ = 91◦

==PAGE:51==
Chapter 5
Assessment of Transfer Function Uncertainty
This chapter presents uncertainty modelling of transfer functions using theory which to a large extent is
based on [Soares, 1991]. In this chapter however, the theory is extended from use of linear regression to
include quadratic regression analysis to improve the quality of obtained results. In addition, results from
modelling the uncertainty term as a quadratic equation, instead of constant and linear, is presented.
The transfer functions derived in last chapter indicates that the amplitude of the motions induced by
waves of unit amplitude on the ship, and the transfer functions depends only on the encounter fre-
quency between ship and wave. As seen throughout this report, transfer functions can be obtained
both experimentally and theoretical. In the ideal world they would be exactly identically - in reality
they are not. The full-scale measured RAO’s are considered to be the true solution, where theoretical
derived RAO’s is a modelled estimate of the real case. Of course, full-scale measured RAO’s always
contain uncertainties such as wrongly calibrated measurement equipment, tolerances on measurement
equipment, etc. But the full-scale obtained RAO’s are assumed to be as close as possible to the real solution.
Figure 5.1a shows an example of a comparison between full-scale measured and theoretical derived RAO.
The full-scale results seems to fall equally often to both sides of the theoretical curve, and the diﬀerences
between the predictions are likely due to measurement error, with a mean value close to zero. In this
case the theoretical model is said to be unbiased. Another situation is shown on ﬁgure 5.1b on which
the theoretical curve follows the tendency of the full-scale points very well but lies above them with a
more of less constant distance in the whole frequency range. If the theoretical results were multiplied by a
constant factor φ, it would be possible to have the theoretical line adjusted to coincide with the full-scale
results and thus an unbiased prediction. The diﬀerence between the theoretical curve and the adjusted
one represents a systematic error in the theory, also known as the model error or model uncertainty.
If the model error is known, the theory can be improved by compensating for the eﬀect of the model
error and thus still be used. The example in ﬁgure 5.1c shows another type of error which, unlike the
constant model error, depends on the wave frequency. This error can be seen to have a linear relation
with frequency, i.e. the error is larger at low frequencies and decreases as frequency increases. The ﬁnal
example of model error is shown on ﬁgure 5.1d. This ﬁgure shows that model error could be a quadratic
function of frequency. The theory is having correct predictions at low and high frequencies, but fail to
predict in frequencies between.
Observation of these typical cases suggest the representation of the model uncertainty of the theoretical
predictions as a factor φ that depends on encounter frequency !e and that multiplies the theoretical
predictions:
39
==PAGE:52==
!e
RAO
Theory
Full-Scale
(a) Unbiased prediction
!e

(b) Constant model error

Figure 5.1: Illustration of unbiased prediction and of a constant, a linear and quadratic type of model
error in predictions.
ˆH(!e) = φ(!e) · H(!e) + ✏
(5.0.1)
where
ˆ
H(!e) represent full-scale RAO values, H(!e) the theoretical predictions, and ✏is the measurement
error (also called residual) which is random and has a zero mean value. The model uncertainty factor has
the general form:
φ(!e) = a + b!e + c!2
e
(5.0.2)
with a,b and c being constants.
It is already stated, transfer functions depends on at least the relative wave heading to the ship, ship
speed and loading condition of the ship i.e. draft. The idealization of the model uncertainty factor is
therefore extended to account for the dependency of the three variables. The constant in equation 5.0.2
thus becomes a function of relative wave heading µ, ship speed V and draft D:
φ(µ, V, D, !e) = a(µ, V, D, !e) + b(µ, V, D)!e + c(µ, V, D)!2
e
(5.0.3)
The model uncertainty factor as a function of all the variables indicated will have to be found through
analysis of data. The number of parameters to be estimated increases the amount of data necessary to
obtain statistical signiﬁcance. As the number of time periods are limited, the simplest idealization of the
model error is considered to begin with.
40

5.1
Frequency-Independent Model Uncertainty Factor (FI)
The simplest idealization of the model uncertainty factor of the transfer functions is obtained by setting
b and c in equation 5.0.3 to zero, giving the encounter frequency independent model uncertainty factor
φ(µ, V, D) = a(µ, V, D).

of measurement and n is the total number of measurements. It should be noted that the measurement
errors are assumed to be independent of encounter frequencies.

5.4
Examples
Figure 5.2 shows two examples of the model uncertainty factor that can be applied to theoretical computed
RAO’s. The model uncertainty factor φ is simply determined by applying equations 5.1.3, 5.2.5, 5.2.6,
5.3.6, 5.3.7 and 5.3.8 to theoretical transfer functions from HydroSTAR and transfer functions obtained
from full-scale measurements. A better estimate of the full-scale RAO’s is then obtained by multiplying
the computed model uncertainty factor φ with the theoretical transfer functions. The results are seen on
ﬁgure 5.2 for two random time periods with the model uncertainty factor being calculated using both FI,
LFD and QFD models. Both plots shows that the new theoretical RAO’s are improved when compared
to full-scale RAO’s. The FI model shows better results in the low frequency range on ﬁgure 5.2a, but
similar results in the remaining range when compared to HydroSTAR. The ﬁgure also shows that applying
the LFD and QFD models improve the HydroSTAR results signiﬁcantly when comparing to full-scale.
Improvements can especially be seen at low frequencies on both ﬁgure 5.2a and 5.2b, and in the 0.7 to 1.0
frequency range on ﬁgure 5.2b.

5.6. REGRESSION ANALYSIS
5.6
Regression Analysis
The calculations in this section are based on regression theory presented in [Kahane, 2008].
The model uncertainty factor coeﬃcients computed in table 5.1, 5.2, 5.3 and 5.4 all belongs to a speciﬁc
combination of vessel speed, draft and relative wave heading. Thus model uncertainty factor coeﬃcients
are not particularly useful individually. However, the data in each table can be used to quantify the eﬀect
of draft, vessel speed and relative wave heading on the model uncertainty factor for each response by use
of regression analysis.
The fundamental goal of regression analysis is to use data to analyse relationships, in this case the
relationship between the model uncertainty factor and the three operational parameters: draft, vessel
speed and relative wave heading. The simplest way to model the relationship between the model error
and the three parameters is a linear one, from which the model error φ can be estimated:
ˆφ = β0 + β1 · D + β2 · µ + β3 · V
(5.6.1)
where β0, β1, β2 and β3 are coeﬃcients which can be estimated by applying the least-squares method on
the data in table 5.1, 5.2, 5.3 and 5.4, which is done by determining the beta coeﬃcients that minimizes
the residuals1. The residual is the diﬀerence between an actual value φi and the estimated or expected
value predicted by the regression model ˆφi (ﬁgure 5.3). The residual is denoted ei and represent all the
factors that the model does not take into account and is related to φi and ˆφi as:
φi = ˆφi + ei
(5.6.2)
ei
φi
ˆφi
φ
V, D, µ
Figure 5.3: Deﬁnition of residual.
A summary of the results from the linear regression analysis are seen in table 5.5 for all responses2, together
with the corresponding coeﬃcient of determination, also named R2. The coeﬃcient of determination is
simply a measure that tells what proportion of φ is explained by draft, speed and relative wave heading.
The value of the coeﬃcient can vary between zero and one. A value of 1 means that the variation in φ
is 100 percent described by draft, speed and relative wave heading (zero residuals). A value of zero on
the other hand would mean the opposite. In addition to checking the coeﬃcient of determination, the
residuals plot have to be controlled to secure a non-biased solution.
1Beta coeﬃcients are determined using MATLAB in this thesis.
2Due to the LFD and QFD models dependency on the encounter frequency !e, regression analysis is conducted at each
frequency. The results in table 5.5 are for encounter frequency !e = 0.66

The results in table 5.5 shows very low R2 values for several of the responses and uncertainty models.
The linear regression for heave model error shows a R2 of 0.34 for the FI model, 0.09 for the LFD and
0.11 for the QFD models. The linear regression of vertical acceleration model uncertainty factor also
shows very low R2 squares values. The regression of pitch and vertical bending moment shows higher
quality results for LFD and QFD model error, but low R2 values for the FI model. When examining
the ﬁtting plot and residual plots for heave LFR model on ﬁgure 5.4a, the low quality of the linear
regression model is easily seen. The linear regression model is seen to overestimate the model uncertainty
factor in most cases (red cross vs blue dots), and the residuals are thus mostly negatives. The linear
regression ﬁtting and residual plot for the other responses and model errors can be seen in appendix D. A
good quality regression model that produces unbiased predictions will have residual plots with mean of zero.
The linear regression shows poor results for almost all responses and all types of models. This indicates
that the relation between model uncertainty factor and draft, vessel speed and relative wave heading are
not linear. The ﬁtting plot seen on ﬁgure 5.4a shows that the model error could seem to follow a quadratic
relation to draft, speed and relative wave heading. The obvious would be testing a quadratic regression
model. The multivariate quadratic regression model with three parameters(variates) for estimating the
model uncertainty factor is deﬁned as:
ˆφ = β0 + β1 · D + β2 · µ + β3 · V + β4 · D · µ + β5 · D · V + β6 · µ · V + β7 · D2 + β8 · µ2 + β9 · V 2 (5.6.3)
where β0,β1,β2,β3,β4,β5,β6,β7,β8 and β9 are regression coeﬃcients which are estimated by use of least-
squares method, exactly like the linear regression model.
The results of the quadratic regression can be seen in table 5.6. The results show increased R2 values
for all of the responses and model error types and the ﬁtting and residual plot on ﬁgure 5.4b shows
better results. The ﬁtting plot shows a better prediction in model error compared to the linear regression
ﬁtting plot on ﬁgure 5.4a. The residual plots also show a nice equally distribution of points above and
below 0, which could indicate a mean of zero. The results for LFD and QFD in table 5.4b is only for
!e = 0.66. Tables containing quadratic regression coeﬃcients for all the considered frequencies can be
found in appendix E.

==PAGE:65==
Chapter 6
Results
Three quadratic regression models for predicting the theoretical model uncertainty factor as a function
of draft, vessel speed and relative heading have been established for following responses: heave, pitch,
vertical bending moment and vertical acceleration. The three models utilize respectively: a frequency
independent, linear frequency dependent and quadratic frequency dependent model uncertainty factor.
This section presents the results from applying these model uncertainty factors to theoretical calculated
transfer functions from HydroSTAR and I-Ship. The model uncertainty factor is simply applied by
multiplying the factor with theoretical transfer function values, i.e.:
Hm(!e) = ˆφ(!e) · H(!e)
(6.0.1)
where ˆφ(!e) is the model uncertainty factor estimated by the quadratic regression model, H(!e) is the
theoretical transfer function calculated by either HydroSTAR or I-ship, and Hm(!e) will be the improved
theoretical transfer function from applying the uncertainty factor from model m. For instance, the
theoretical transfer functions H(!e) computed for heave at a given draft D, speed V and relative wave
heading µ can be improved using of the FI model by multiplying the estimated model uncertainty factor
ˆφ from the quadratic regression analysis:
HF D(!e) = ˆφ · H(!e)
(6.0.2)
with ˆφ determined from the quadratic regression analysis as:
ˆφ = −463.6 + 72.2D −0.7µ −2.7V + 0.1DV + 0.2µV −2.8D2 −0.001µ2 −0.003V 2
(6.0.3)
The eﬀects from applying the model uncertainty factor on theoretical transfer functions can be seen seen for
a few selected time periods on ﬁgure 6.1 for heave motion, ﬁgure 6.2 for pitch motion, ﬁgure 6.3 for vertical
bending moment and ﬁgure 6.4 for vertical acceleration. The ﬁgures shows a general improvement of
theoretical transfer functions. Specially ﬁgure 6.1a and 6.1e shows signiﬁcant improvement for theoretical
heave transfer functions at low frequencies. The model uncertainty factors eﬀect on pitch motion transfer
functions can been seen to be prominent at relative wave headings close to 90 degree on ﬁgure 6.2c. The
transfer functions for vertical bending moment on ﬁgure 6.3 shows a major improvement when applying
the LFD and QFD model uncertainty factors. Figure 6.4 also clearly shows the eﬀect of applying the
model uncertainty factors to the theoretical transfer functions. Specially ﬁgure 6.4c shows a large correction.
The plots clearly shows how applying the model uncertainty factors improves the theoretical predictions
in most cases. However, a method for quantifying the results of the model adjustments needs to be
53
==PAGE:66==
established. This quantiﬁcation can be accomplished by use of the least-squares method, where the sum
of squares of the error between two transfer functions are calculated as:
S =
n
X
i=1
✏2
i =
n
X
i=1
( ˆHi −Hm,i)2
(6.0.4)
with ˆH being measured full-scale transfer function and Hm being the adjusted theoretical transfer function
using either a FI, LFD or QFD model uncertainty factor.
Table 6.1 shows the results from applying this least-squares method to theoretical transfer function and
improved/adjusted theoretical transfer functions. The results are computed for all the studied time periods
shown in table 2.3 and averaged for each date.
The results shows that the biggest errors between full-scale and HydroSTAR are seen for heave and
vertical acceleration with a total error of 1.358 and 1.556 respectively. The error between full-scale and
HydroSTAR is signiﬁcant lower for pitch with a total errror of 0.045, and 0.631 between full-scale and
I-Ship for vertical bending moment.
Applying the FI model uncertainty factor to HydroSTAR RAO’s reduces the total error from 1.358 to
0.785 for heave, 0.045 to 0.018 for pitch, 0.631 to 0.598 for vertical bending moment and from 1.556 to
0.918 for vertical acceleration. Or in terms of percentage reduction: 42%, 60%, 5% and 41% for heave,
pitch, vertical bending moment and vertical acceleration respectively.
The LFD model uncertainty factor yields a even better result. The total error are seen to drop from 1.358
to 0.497 for heave, 0.045 to 0.014 for pitch, 0.631 to 0.280 for vertical bending moment and 1.556 to 0.410
for vertical acceleration. In percentage reduction: 63%, 69%, 56% and 74%.
Multiplying the HydroSTAR transfer functions with the QFD model uncertainty factor decreases the
total error from 1.358 to 0.553 for heave, 0.045 to 0.014 for pitch, 0.631 to 0.198 vertical bending moment
and from 1.556 to 0.329 for vertical acceleration. In percentage, the reductions are respectively 60%, 69%,
69% and 79%.
In general the total error is seen to be reduced as the model is increased from FI to LFD and QFD.
However, applying the LFD model to theoretical heave transfer functions shows a better results than
applying the QFD model.

Chapter 7
Discussion
7.1
Interpolation of RAO
The transfer functions provided for this project was calculated at a discrete set of drafts, vessel speeds
and relative wave headings. The RAO’s were computed for three drafts: 10.5m, 12.5m and 14.5m, with
the RAO for 14.5m being the only set used in this project. This was decided due to all voyages being
with a draft of at least 14 meters. In terms of speed and relative wave heading, the RAO’s were computed
from 5 knot to 25 knot in steps of 5 knot and for angles of 10◦from 0◦to 360◦. It was decided to perform
linear interpolation for voyages with vessel speed and relative wave heading in between these discrete
calculated transfer functions. A simpler solution would have been using the nearest calculated RAO
instead of interpolation. The diﬀerence in results between interpolating transfer functions or round to
nearest transfer function are seen on ﬁgure 7.1 for theoretical heave motion transfer function for time
period 181 on September 20th. The ﬁgure shows, for heave at least, that there is a big diﬀerence in
amplitude between computed transfer functions at µ = 100◦and µ = 110◦whereas the diﬀerence in
amplitude between V = 10kn and V = 15kn is almost neglectable. This shows that not interpolating
between relative wave headings will yields a signiﬁcant uncertainty in the ﬁnal results.
ωe [rad/s]

0.5

61
==PAGE:74==
7.2. RELATIVE WAVE HEADING
7.2
Relative wave heading
The relative wave heading which is determined by the WaMoS wave radar has been assumed to be
correct throughout this report. This section will examine how an uncertainty in the estimate of relative
wave heading will aﬀect the derived full-scale transfer functions. Figure 7.2 shows how the encounter
wave energy spectrum will change if the relative wave heading is changed by -10◦, -5◦, +5◦and +10◦
respectively. The ﬁgure shows that peak frequency is decreases as relative wave heading is lowered and
increases as relative wave heading is increased. The peak amplitude of the wave energy spectrum increases
as relative wave heading is decreased and decreases if relative wave heading is increased. The change in
derived full-scale transfer function as results of change in encounter wave energy spectrum can be seen
on ﬁgure 7.3. The ﬁgure shows than an uncertainty in relative wave heading of -+10 degree can give an
amplitude diﬀerence of approximately 0.4-0.5. How this relative wave heading uncertainty aﬀects the
ﬁnal results is hard to quantify, since a change in relative wave heading also would change the theoretical
transfer functions used for comparison in the uncertainty modelling in chapter 5. A further study of this
relative wave heading would have to be conducted.

CHAPTER 7. DISCUSSION
7.3
L-value
The level of smoothing in the Fast Fourier Transformation of the time history signal to spectrum was
controlled by the window size L in the matlab package WAFO. In this project, a smoothing value of
L = 400 was chosen for all calculations. Increasing or decreasing the L value will change the computed
response spectrum as seen on ﬁgure 7.4. The ﬁgure shows how a too low L value smooths the response
spectrum, and potentially removes information. The problem here is: for which window size is the optimal
spectra achieved?. Unfortunately, there is no general answer to this question, and the results therefore
rely on manual choices. However, ﬁgure 7.4 and ﬁgure 7.5 shows that changing the window size mainly
changes the "spikyness" of the response spectrum and transfer function and not the overall shape - as
long as the window size isn’t set too low, as L = 100 shows.

7.4
Model Uncertainty Factor
In this thesis, the transfer functions model uncertainty factor was modelled in three diﬀerent ways:
a frequency independent, a linear frequency dependent and a quadratic frequency dependent model.
Equations 5.1.3, 5.2.5, 5.2.6, 5.3.6, 5.3.7 and 5.3.8 showed that the complexity of the equations used
63
==PAGE:76==
7.5. REGRESSION MODELS
for determining the model coeﬃcients quickly increased to be immense as the model uncertainty factor
became more advanced. The results also showed that in most cases, the most advanced type of modelling
the uncertainty factor also gave the best results. But even though the QFD model showed excellent results
in most cases, a few limitations was observed. For instance, ﬁgure 6.3b and 6.3c shows how the peak is
impossible to model, if the original theoretical transfer function did not include such peak. In this case, a
cubic or even quartic frequency dependent model could potentially provide better results.
7.5
Regression Models
The regression models in this thesis was limited to linear and quadratic regression models. The linear
regression model showed limited use due to very low coeﬃcients of determination (table 5.5) and poor
looking residual plots (ﬁgure 5.4a). The quadratic regression model showed very good results with high
coeﬃcients of determination (table 5.6) and correct looking residual plots (ﬁgure 5.4b). Even though good
results were obtained, more regression models such as exponential or cubic could have been considered, to
improve the accuracy of the model even further.
7.6
Closed-form or Numerical calculation of Model Uncertainty Coeﬃcients
The estimation of the a,b and c coeﬃcients in the model uncertainty in chapter 5 are all done by use of
the derived closed-form equations. The coeﬃcients can also be determined by solving equations 5.1.2,
5.2.3, 5.2.4, 5.3.3, 5.3.4 and 5.3.5 numerically. This can for instance be done by use of Matlab’s build in
fsolve function. Table 7.1 shows the least-squares results for heave motion as seen in table 6.1 next to
results where model uncertainty coeﬃcients are numerically determined. The results are seen to be nearly
identical, with closed-form being a small winner in terms of the optimal method.

CHAPTER 7. DISCUSSION
7.7
From Manually to Real-time
All calculations in this project have been conducted using the numerical tool Matlab. To minimize the
amount of manual work or iterations in calculations, a system of programs have been created to perform
all calculations described throughout the report. Figure 7.6 shows the complete list of Matlab programs
created and how they interact. The main script import response time histories together with theoretical
computed transfer functions and information about draft. Information stationary time periods are entered
together with response of interest and user determined L value (see WAFO section). Based on these
input, the main program sends information on stationary time periods to the WaveSpecWamos script that
returns encounter wave spectrum and information about speed and relative wave heading for each time
period. The main script then computes the full-scale transfer functions and export a data ﬁle containing
full-scale transfer functions, relative wave heading, draft, ship speed, encounter frequencies, and theoretical
transfer functions for each of the user-deﬁned stationary time periods and responses. This data ﬁle is
then sent to the uncertainty program which, based on user-input, calculate and export the model error
coeﬃcients in a text ﬁle with relative wave heading, vessel speed and draft for each time period. The
regression program then import the uncertainty data and perform a quadratic regression analysis, with
regression beta coeﬃcients as output. A ﬁnal script, named comparison, then import both full-scale and
theoretical transfer functions together with regression beta coeﬃcients used for adjusting the theoretical
transfer functions to be more accurate.
This entire process could potentially be fully automated. An advantage of automating this process could
be increased statistical accuracy as more data was collected. The workﬂow for such automated process
can be seen on ﬁgure 7.7. In this workﬂow, WaMoS data are imported into the WaveSpecWamos program
which automatically could determine stationary time periods and forward wave spectra, ship speed and
relative wave heading for such periods to the main program. The main program then import the response
time histories for the stationary time periods, together with draft data and theoretical transfer functions.
The main program automatically computes full-scale transfer functions and export data as shown on ﬁgure
7.7. The uncertainty program determines the model error coeﬃcients which are sent to the regression
program for determination of regression beta coeﬃcients. Finally, a program imports the theoretical
transfer functions together with the regression coeﬃcients and calculate new improved theoretical transfer
functions. These improved theoretical transfer functions can then be imported into various decision
support systems for further use.

==PAGE:81==
Chapter 8
Conclusion
Transfer functions have been derived for a large container ship using full-scale measurements provided
by the TULCS project. The transfer functions were derived for heave, roll and pitch motion, as well
as vertical bending moment and vertical acceleration. The derived transfer functions were considered
to be valid in the frequency range from approximately !e = 0.4 to 2 radians per second. The wave
energy spectra used for deriving full-scale transfer functions showed signs of missing information at low
frequencies, which gave poor transfer functions results for frequencies below !e = 0.4. A cut-oﬀfrequency
were therefore introduced to ignore the lower frequencies in further analysis.
The derived full-scale transfer functions were compared to theoretical calculated transfer functions pro-
vided by Bureau Veritas. The uncertainty between full scale transfer functions and theoretical transfer
functions were assessed. The uncertainty between the transfer functions were quantiﬁed by use of a model
uncertainty factor, which were calculated for the transfer functions belonging to each of the studied
time period. Three diﬀerent model uncertainty factors were studied: a frequency independent, a linear
frequency dependent and a quadratic frequency dependent model.
The eﬀect of draft, speed and relative wave heading on the model uncertainty factors was studied by use
of a quadratic regression analysis. An expression for the three model uncertainty factors as functions of
draft, speed and relative wave heading was determined.
The three model uncertainty factors were used to improve the theoretical calculated transfer functions. All
of the three model factors were found to decrease the total sum of error between full-scale and theoretical
transfer functions.
Finally, this method can be implemented in any decision support system to improve prediction of responses.
69

%% Response Amplitude Operator (RAO)
257
258
WSspecOm_enc = (omega_enc.^4./g^2).*WspecOm_enc; % Wave slope spectrum
259
k = omega_enc.^2./g; % wave number
260
261
RAO_heave = sqrt(interp1(S_Rs_heave.w,S_Rs_heave.S,omega_enc)./WspecOm_enc);
262
RAO_roll = sqrt(interp1(S_Rs_roll.w,S_Rs_roll.S,omega_enc)./WSspecOm_enc);
263
RAO_pitch = sqrt(interp1(S_Rs_pitch.w,S_Rs_pitch.S,omega_enc)./WSspecOm_enc);
264
RAO_vbm = sqrt(interp1(S_Rs_vbm.w,S_Rs_vbm.S,omega_enc)./WspecOm_enc);
265
RAO_acc = sqrt(interp1(S_Rs_heave.w,S_Rs_acc,omega_enc)./WspecOm_enc);

[A] = diffmatrix_1d_uniform(1,length(data_heave),data_time(2)-data_time(1),1,1);

function [omega_enc,WspecOm_enc,Vship,RelHead] = ExtractWaveSpecWaMos(sDate,timeperiod,g)
2
3
%% *****************************************************************************
4
% ExtractWaveSpecWaMos:
5
%
Extract data from WaMos data files and compute ship velocity,
6
%
relative heading, encounter frequencies and encounter wave energy spectrum.
7
%
8
% Syntax :
9
%
[omega_enc,WspecOm_enc,Vship,Relhead] = ExtractWaveSpecWaMos(sDate,timeperiod)
10
%
11
% Input
:
12
%
sDate
: Date of data
13
%
timeperiod
: Timeperiod of the date
14
%
15
% Output :
16
%
omega_enc
: Encounter frequencies
17
%
WspecOm_enc
: Encounter wave energy spectrum
18
%
Vship
: Vessel velocity
19
%
Relhead
: Relative heading
20
%
21
%
22
% Author
: Kasper Fonss Bach (based on work by Ulrik Dam Nielsen)
23
% Date
: <<date>>
24
% Version : 1.0
25
%*******************************************************************************
26
27
% clear all; close all;
28
% g = 9.81;
29
% timeperiod = 182;
30
%sDate='August 12th';

%sDate='August 20th';
32
%sDate='September 16th';
33
% sDate='September 20th';
34
%sDate='October 02nd';
35

%% Read data from file
37
%Files = dir(fullfile(['C:\Users\s072797\Dropbox\Dokumenter\DTU\Speciale\Data\Waves\',sDate,'\*.ftm']));
38
Files = dir(fullfile('/Users/kasperbach/Dropbox/dokumenter/dtu/Speciale/Data/Waves',sDate,'/*.ftm'));
39
40
41
for i=1:length(Files)
42
WamosFile=[Files(i).name]; % File name, as a string, of file no. i
43
%filename = ['C:\Users\s072797\Dropbox\Dokumenter\DTU\Speciale\Data\Waves\',sDate,'\',WamosFile];
44
filename = ['/Users/kasperbach/Dropbox/dokumenter/dtu/Speciale/Data/Waves/',sDate,'/',WamosFile];
45
46
47
%% Extract (true) wave frequencies
48
NoF=64;
% Number of discrete frequencies
49
fid=fopen(filename);
% open wamosfile
50
file=textscan(fid,'%f',NoF,'HeaderLines',151);
% read the 64 discrete frequecies from line 151 in
51
Freqs=file{:}; % Frequencies in Hz
52
53
%% Extract mean relative wave heading
54
NoD=90;
% Number of discrete directions
55
frewind(fid);
119
==PAGE:132==
G.2. WAVESPEC
56
file=textscan(fid,'%f','HeaderLines',155);
% Directional spectral components
57
58
frewind(fid);
59
file2=textscan(fid,'%*s %f',1,'HeaderLines',27);
60
GPScourse=file2{1};
% GPS course [deg.] (relative to North)
61
Mus(i)=GPScourse;
62
frewind(fid);
63
file2=textscan(fid,'%*s %f',1,'HeaderLines',130);
64
WaveDir=file2{1};
% Mean wave direction [deg.] (coming from, relativ
65
Mu(i)=WaveDir;
66
67
frewind(fid);
68
file3=textscan(fid,'%*s %f',1,'HeaderLines',28);
69
Vs=file3{1};
% Ship speed in water [knots]
70
Vship(i)=Vs*0.5144444;
% convert to [m/s]
71
Vship_kt(i)= Vs;
72
73
Relhead=180-(WaveDir-GPScourse);
% Relative mean wave direction [deg.]
74
if(Relhead>360)
75
Relhead=Relhead-360;
76
elseif(Relhead<0)
77
Relhead=Relhead+360;
78
end
79
80
if Relhead > 180
81
RelHead(i) = (180-(Relhead-180));
82
else
83
RelHead(i) = Relhead;
84
end
85

87
%% Set directional wave spectrum [m^2/(rad Hz)]
88
89
NoF=NoF+1;
90
for j=1:NoD
91
indicies=((j-1)*NoF+1:j*NoF-1)+1;
92
WspecDir(:,j,i)=file{1}(indicies);
93
end
94
95
%% Calculate 1-D frequency wave spectrum
96
97
Ddir=4;
% Directional spacing [deg.]
98
Dfreq=0.0055;
% Frequency spacing [Hz]
99
Scaling=Ddir*pi/180;
% Directional spacing [rad.]
100
NoF=NoF-1;
101
for k=1:NoF
% loop over Number of Frequencies
102
spectra(k)=trapz(WspecDir(k,:,i))*Scaling;
% integration over all directions for each frequency
103
end
104
105
Domega = (Dfreq*2*pi);
106
omega = 2*pi*Freqs; % convert frequencies to angular frequencies
107
108
WspecFreq(:,i) = spectra';
109
110
WspecOm(:,i) = WspecFreq(:,i)*(Dfreq/Domega);
% Convert from frequency to angular frequency
111
112
%% Calculate significant wave height and zero crossing period.
113
114
m0=trapz(omega,WspecOm(:,i));
% 0th order spectral moment
120
==PAGE:133==
APPENDIX G. MATLAB CODE
115
m2= trapz(omega,omega.^2.*WspecOm(:,i));
% 2nd order spectral moment
116
m4= trapz(omega,omega.^4.*WspecOm(:,i));
% 2nd order spectral moment
117
118
epsilon(i) = sqrt(1-(m2^2/(m0*m4)));
% bandwidth parameter
119
120
Hs(i)=4*sqrt(1-(epsilon(i)^2/2))*sqrt(m0);
% Significant wave height(equation 4.18 in Nielse
121
122
Tz(i)=2*pi*sqrt(m0/m2);
% Mean zero crossing period
123
124
125
%% Calculate encounter wave frequencies
126
127
omega_enc(:,i) = omega-((omega.^2./g).*Vship(i).*cosd(RelHead(i))); % encounter frequencies
128
129
%% 1-D encounter frequency wave spectrum
130
131
WspecOm_enc(:,i) = WspecOm(:,i).*(g./abs(g-2.*omega.*Vship(i).*cosd(RelHead(i)))); % encounter wave sp
132
133
fclose(fid);
134
end
135

%% Statistics of significant wave height (Hs)
137
138
for i=2:length(Hs)-1
139
Std20_Hs(i) = std([Hs(i-1),Hs(i),Hs(i+1)]);
140
Mean20_Hs(i) = mean([Hs(i-1),Hs(i),Hs(i+1)]);
141
CoV20_Hs(i) = Std20_Hs(i)/Mean20_Hs(i);
142
end
143
144
%% Statistics of zero-upcrossing period (Tz)

147
Std20_Tz(i) = std([Tz(i-1),Tz(i),Tz(i+1)]);
148
Mean20_Tz(i) = mean([Tz(i-1),Tz(i),Tz(i+1)]);
149
CoV20_Tz(i) = Std20_Tz(i)/Mean20_Tz(i);
150
end
151
152
%% Statistics of mean (true) wave direction (Mu)
153
154
for i=2:length(RelHead)-1
155
Std20_RelHead(i) = std([RelHead(i-1),RelHead(i),RelHead(i+1)]);
156
Mean20_RelHead(i) = mean([RelHead(i-1),RelHead(i),RelHead(i+1)]);
157
end
158
159
%% Statistics of ship speed (Vship)
160
161
for i=2:length(Vship)-1
162
Std20_Vship_kt(i) = std([Vship_kt(i-1),Vship_kt(i),Vship_kt(i+1)]);
163
Mean20_Vship_kt(i) = mean([Vship_kt(i-1),Vship_kt(i),Vship_kt(i+1)]);
164
CoV20_Vship_kt(i) = Std20_Vship_kt(i)/Mean20_Vship_kt(i);
165
end

% figure(101)

% subplot(3,1,1)
171

172
% ylabel('Mean [m]','interpreter','latex','fontsize',20)
173
% title('Significant wave height, $H_s$ [m]','interpreter','latex','fontsize',20)

% plot(Std20_Hs(2:end),'linewidth', 1.5)

% phi = a
86

87

fun1 = @(a) sum(-2.*(RAO_filtered-a.*tRAO_filtered).*tRAO_filtered); % numerical estimate of a
89
a1_num(k) = fsolve(fun1,-10)

% phi(omega) = a+b*omega

95

96

97
b_5 = sum(omega_enc_filtered.^2.*tRAO_filtered.^2);
98
99

a2(k) = (b_4-b2(k)*b_3)/b_2; % closed-form extimate of a

103

b2_num(k) = x(2);

% phi(omega) = a+b*omega+c*omega^2

123
